6 min
The Hanafi Concept of Azimah: Definition and Legal Effect
Defines azimah with legal effects and example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of azimah, apply its principle to the case of zihar, and distinguish between definitive and speculative rulings.

Core Idea
Azimah is the firm original ruling; a firm resolve can be treated as the act itself in Hanafi law.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, the author defines azimah both linguistically and technically, and illustrates its application with the example of zihar. Understanding azimah is fundamental to grasping how original rulings operate before any concessions. The passage also touches on the epistemological weight of rulings—denying a definitively established azimah can lead to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition
The text first defines azimah as 'intention when it reaches utmost firmness' (العزيمة: هي القصد إذا كان في نهاية الوكادة). Recognize this as the linguistic meaning.
This sets the stage for the technical Shar'i meaning.
2
Understand the technical Shar'i definition
In Shariah, azimah refers to what is binding upon us from the rulings initially, without any excuse. It is called azimah because of the extreme firmness of its cause—the command of Allah.
Contrast with rukhsah (concession) which relaxes the ruling due to an excuse.
3
Analyze the example of determination in zihar
The text says that in zihar, the determination to have intercourse is treated as a return (to the permissible state), requiring expiation. This shows that a firm resolve can have legal consequences.
This is a unique Hanafi position.
4
Examine the classification of rulings based on certainty
The commentary discusses that denying a definitively established (qat'i al-thubut) ruling may lead to disbelief, while denying a nearly definitive ruling leads to fisq, and a speculative ruling leads to disobedience.
This highlights the importance of epistemological weight.
5
Recognize the madhhab dispute
The text implicitly supports the Hanafi view that determination can be treated as the act. The Shafi'i school generally requires actual action for legal consequences.
The practical difference arises in cases like zihar and oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزيمة والرخصة:
العزيمة: هي القصد إذا كان في نهاية الوكادة.
ولهذا قلنا: إن العزم على الوَطْء عَوْدٌ في باب الظهار؛ لأنه كالموجود، فجاز أن يعتبر
العزم
موجوداً عند قيام الدلالة، ولهذا لو قال أعزمُ يكون حالفاً، وفي الشرع: عبارةٌ عما
لَزِمَنا من الأحكام ابتداء، سُمِّيت عزيمة؛ لأنها في غاية الوكادة لوكادة سببها، وهو كونُ أي بلا عارض
العزيمة
الآمر مفترض الطاعة بحكم أنه إلهنا ونحن عبيده.
أي الأمر
ولا يُعاقب إلخ: إذا لم يكن الترك مقروناً بالإنكار، وإلا فقد يكفر لو كان قطعي الثبوت، ويفسق في القريب منه ويكون عاصيا في الظني، ولم يذكر المباح لعدم شموله في سلك الإحكام المكلف بإتيانها العبد، وحكمه: أنه لا يثاب ولا يعاقب بفعله ولا بتركه، وقد يكفر يجحده أيضاً إذا قطع بثبوته كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا، و لم يذكر المكروه تنزيهاً وتحريماً والحرام؛ لأنها تعريف بمقايسة الفرض والواجب والسنة والنفل كذا في "الحصول". نظيران: أي مثلان في كون كل منهما زائداً شرعاً لنا لا علينا، إلا أنه كما أن النفل اسم للزيادة فكذا التطوع اسم لإتيان خير يؤتى به عن طوع كذا في "المعدن". إن العزم على الخ: يعني المظاهر إذا عزم على الوطء كأنه عاد إلى حالة الإباحة، حتى وجبت الكفارة عليه.
عند قيام الدلالة: ولذا يقرب أثر العزم الجازم المصمم أثر الفعل كالعزم على الزنا يأثم به وإن كان صغيرة لا كبيرة كحقيقة كما ورد العينان تزنيان إلخ، وقوله : ولهذا إلخ أي يراد بالعزم نفس الفعل المعزوم عليه؛ لكمال قربه منه واتصاله به تجوزاً كأنه قال: أو جده كذا في "الحصول". وكذا في الشافي ولأن العزيمة هي القصد المؤكد صار العزم يميناً. [الشافي: ص ۳۱۱]
Chapter on Azimah and Rukhsah: Azimah is intention when it reaches utmost firmness. Hence we say: the determination to have intercourse is a return (to permissible state) in the chapter of zihar because it is like the actual act. So it is permissible to consider determination as existing when there is evidence. Thus if someone says 'I determine', it becomes an oath. In Shariah, it is an expression of what is binding upon us from the rulings initially. It is called azimah because of the extreme firmness of its cause, which is that the Commander is obligated to be obeyed by virtue of being our God and we are His servants. And one is not punished... if the abandonment is not accompanied by disapproval; otherwise it may become disbelief if the proof is definitive, and sinful if the proof is close to definitive, and disobedience if the proof is speculative. And he did not mention mubah because it is not included in the rulings that the servant is charged with performing. Its ruling: that one is neither rewarded nor punished for doing it or leaving it. And one may become a disbeliever by denying it if its establishment is definitive, as in His saying: 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt'...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on Azimah (determination/obligation) and Rukhsah (concession). The text first defines Azimah linguistically as firm intention, then provides a legal application: in the case of zihar (a form of divorce by analogy), if the husband determines to have intercourse, that determination is treated like the actual act, requiring the expiation. This illustrates the principle that a firm resolve can have legal consequences. In Shariah, Azimah refers to the original rulings that are binding on us without any impediment or excuse. The commentary then discusses the ruling of various categories of acts: if an act is definitively proven, denying it can lead to disbelief; if nearly definitive, it leads to fisq; if speculative, it is disobedience. It notes that mubah (permissible) acts have no reward or punishment. The text also clarifies that the definition of Azimah is given in comparison to fard, wajib, sunnah, and nafal. The end discusses the concept of 'azm (resolve) and its similarity to an oath. The reference to the verse about hunting after pilgrimage illustrates that denial of a definitively established matter can lead to disbelief.

Key Terms
العزيمة (al-azimah): The original legal ruling that is binding on a person without any excuse, arising from the command of Allah.
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession that relaxes the original ruling due to an excuse. (Not fully defined in passage, but contextually implied as opposite of azimah).
العزم (al-azm): Firm determination or resolve, which in Hanafi usul can be legally treated as the act itself when supported by evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of denying a ruling that is definitively established (qat'i al-thubut)?
A) It is a minor sin.
B) It may lead to disbelief.
C) It is considered fisq.
D) It is permissible if done with interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Azimah is the original binding ruling; the Hanafi school grants legal effect to firm determination in certain cases, reflecting a broader view of intention's role in law.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Azima and Rukhsah in Hanafi Usul
Defines azima and rukhsah with linguistic analysis and fiqh examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define azima and rukhsah technically, state their categories (fard/wajib for azima; two types for rukhsah), and apply the example of uttering kufr under duress to distinguish concession from permissibility.

Core Idea
Azima is the binding original ruling; rukhsah is a concession that permits the act while the prohibition remains.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi lays foundational definitions for two key categories of legal rulings: azima (original determination) and rukhsah (concession). Understanding these terms is critical for differentiating between rulings that are always binding and those that are temporarily lightened due to excuse. We will explore the linguistic roots, technical meanings, and practical examples the author provides, with special attention to the Hanafi view that rukhsah does not remove the underlying prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical meanings
First, note the linguistic sense of azima (firm intention) and rukhsah (ease). Then extract the Shariah definitions: azima is a ruling binding from the outset; rukhsah is a turning from difficulty to ease due to an excuse.
The author uses the phrase 'عبارة عما لزمنا من الأحكام ابتداء' for azima and 'صرف الأمر من عسر إلى يسر' for rukhsah.
2
Analyze the cause and effect of azima
Understand why azima is strong: because the Commander (Allah) is authoritative. This makes azima divisions (fard, wajib) absolutely binding in normal circumstances.
The cause is 'كون الآمر مفترض الطاعة'.
3
Examine the linguistic illustration of azima
The author shows that firm determination is treated as actual in zihar and oath – this demonstrates how 'azima' works in fiqh: intention with evidence can equate to act.
Example: In zihar, determination to return to one's wife is considered a return, necessitating kaffarah.
4
Categorize rukhsah and its types
Rukhsah reduces to two types: (1) concession of act while prohibition remains. Know the examples: uttering kufr under duress, cursing the Prophet, destroying property, killing – all remain haram but are excused conditionally.
The text says 'رخصة الفعل مع بقاء الحرمة' – the prohibition is not removed.
5
Connect to usul principles
Reflect that this distinction matters for istinbat: when deriving rulings, we must identify whether a text commands azima or permits rukhsah, and whether an excuse applies.
In fatwa, mufti must know which ruling is azima and when rukhsah is triggered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزيمة والرخصة:
العزيمة: هي القصد إذا كان في نهاية الوكادة.
ولهذا قلنا: إن العزم على الوَطْء عَوْدٌ في باب الظهار؛ لأنه كالموجود، فجاز أن يعتبر العزم موجوداً عند قيام الدلالة، ولهذا لو قال أعزمُ يكون حالفاً، وفي الشرع: عبارةٌ عما لَزِمَنا من الأحكام ابتداء، سُمِّيت عزيمة؛ لأنها في غاية الوكادة لوكادة سببها، وهو كونُ الآمر مفترض الطاعة بحكم أنه إلهنا ونحن عبيده.
وأقسام العزيمة ما ذكرنا من الفرض والواجب.
وأما الرخصة: فعبارة عن اليسر والسهولة.
وفي الشرع: صرف الأمر من عُسر إلى يُسر بواسطة عذر في المكلف.
وأَنْواعُها مختلفة لاختلاف أسبابها، وهي أعذار العباد، وفي العاقبة تَؤُولُ إلى نوعين:
أحدهما: رخصة الفعل مع بقاء الحرمة بمنزلة العفو في باب الجناية، وذلك نحو إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان مع اطمئنان القلب عند الإكراه، وسبُّ النبي عليه، وإتلاف مال المسلم، وقتل النفس ظلماً.
Section on Determination and Concession: Determination (azima): It is intention when it is at the utmost firmness. For this reason we say: Indeed, determination to have intercourse constitutes a return in the chapter of zihar because it is like an existing act, so it is permissible to consider determination as existing when there is evidence. For this reason, if one says 'I am determined', he becomes an oath-taker. In the Shariah, it is an expression for what has become binding upon us of rulings from the beginning. It is called azima because it is at the utmost firmness due to the firmness of its cause, which is that the Commander is obligatory to obey by virtue of Him being our God and we His slaves. The divisions of azima are what we mentioned: fard and wajib. As for concession (rukhsah): It is an expression for ease and facility. In the Shariah: turning a matter from difficulty to ease by means of an excuse in the obligated person. Its types differ due to the difference of their causes, which are the excuses of the servants, and ultimately they reduce to two types: One of them: concession of the act while retaining prohibition, analogous to pardon in the chapter of crimes, such as uttering words of disbelief on the tongue while the heart is at ease under duress, cursing the Prophet, destroying the property of a Muslim, and unjustly killing a soul.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi provides formal usul definitions for two key categories of ahkam: azima (determination) and rukhsah (concession). The author first defines azima linguistically as firm intention, then illustrates its legal effect by showing that in zihar, a firm determination to return to one's wife is treated as actual return, and similarly saying 'I am determined' is considered an oath. Technically, azima means the rulings that originally bind us without any intervening factor. Its strength comes from the fact that the Commander (Allah) is absolutely authoritative over us. The divisions of azima are fard and wajib. Then rukhsah is defined as ease and facility, and technically as the shifting of a command from difficulty to ease due to an excuse in the mukallaf. The types of rukhsah are many but ultimately reduce to two: one where the act is permitted but the original prohibition remains, like uttering disbelief under duress while the heart is firm in faith — the act is not truly permissible but is excused. The commentary explains that the prohibition remains because Allah's right in faith is not voided, but the servant is allowed to save his life. Other examples given are cursing the Prophet, destroying a Muslim's property, and killing a soul (under duress where one is forced to kill, but even then it remains sinful unless necessary).

Key Terms
عزيمة (azima): In the Shariah, it refers to rulings that are binding upon us from the beginning, without any intervening excuse.
رخصة (rukhsah): In the Shariah, it is the turning of a matter from difficulty to ease due to an excuse in the mukallaf.
الوكادة (al-wikada): Firmness, strength. The cause of azima is at the utmost firmness because it comes from Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal effect of a firm determination (azima) in the chapter of zihar?
A) It has no legal effect.
B) It is considered like the actual act, so it necessitates the kaffarah.
C) It is ignored unless accompanied by an oath.
D) It constitutes an oath.

Key Takeaway
Azima and rukhsah are two fundamental categories: azima is the binding original ruling (fard/wajib), and rukhsah is a conditional concession that does not remove the underlying prohibition.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
beginner
8 min
Definition of Azima and Rukhsa and Two Types of Rukhsa
Defines azima and rukhsa, then divides rukhsa into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define azima and rukhsa in Hanafi usul, and distinguish between the two types of rukhsa by applying the defining criteria.

Core Idea
Rukhsa is a Sharia concession: either the act remains prohibited but excused, or becomes permissible.

In this passage, the author of Usul al-Shashi lays down the foundational definitions of azima (original ruling) and rukhsa (concession). He then performs a critical operation: dividing rukhsa into two categories based on the effect on the act's original prohibition. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying rulings in cases of duress and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Define azima
State that azima refers to the original ruling without any concession, and it includes fard and wajib as previously mentioned.
Azima is the baseline ruling before considering any excuses.
2
Define rukhsa linguistically and technically
Linguistically, rukhsa means ease and facility. Technically in Sharia, it is the conversion of a ruling from difficulty to ease due to a valid excuse in the obligated person.
The excuse must be present in the mukallaf (responsible person).
3
Recognize the two types of rukhsa
Identify that rukhsa leads to two types: Type 1 where the act remains prohibited but the person is excused (like a pardon), and Type 2 where the act becomes permissible (mubah).
The author emphasizes that in Type 1 the prohibition itself is not lifted, only the punishment.
4
Connect examples to each type
For Type 1, examples include uttering kufr under coercion while the heart is firm, insulting the Prophet, destroying property, or killing unjustly. For Type 2, the example is eating carrion or drinking wine under compulsion or necessity (like starvation).
These examples illustrate the practical difference between the two types.
5
Note the consequence of refusing Type 2
The author states that if a person refrains from the permissible act (Type 2) until death, they become sinful, akin to suicide.
This shows that the Sharia intends preservation of life, making the concession a duty to accept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزيمة والرخصة وأقسام العزيمة ما ذكرنا من الفرض والواجب. وأما الرخصة: فعبارة عن اليسر والسهولة. وفي الشرع: صرف الأمر من عُسر إلى يُسر بواسطة عذر في المكلف. وأَنْواعُها مختلفة لاختلاف أسبابها، وهي أعذار العباد، وفي العاقبة تَؤُولُ إلى نوعين: أحدهما: رخصة الفعل مع بقاء الحرمة بمنزلة العفو في باب الجناية، وذلك نحو إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان مع اطمئنان القلب عند الإكراه، وسبّ النبي عليه، وإتلاف مال المسلم، وقتل النفس ظلماً. والنوع الثاني: تغيير صفة الفعل بأن يصير مباحاً في حقه، قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنِ اضْطُرَّ في مَخْمَصَةٍ﴾ وذلك نحو الإكراه على أكل الميتة، وشرب الخمر. هو الجوع وحكمه أنه لو امتنع عن تناوله حتى قتل يكون آثماً بامتناعه عن المباح، وصار النوع من الرخصة كقاتل نفسه.
Chapter on Azima and Rukhsa. As for the divisions of azima, they are what we mentioned of fard and wajib. As for rukhsa: it is an expression of ease and facility. In the Sharia: it is the conversion of a matter from difficulty to ease due to an excuse in the obligated person. Its types vary due to the difference in their causes, which are the excuses of servants, and ultimately they lead to two types. One: a concession of the act with the prohibition remaining, similar to pardon in the chapter of aggression, such as uttering the word of disbelief on the tongue while the heart is at ease under compulsion, insulting the Prophet (peace be upon him), destroying the property of a Muslim, and killing a person unjustly. The second type: changing the description of the act so that it becomes permissible for him. Allah said: 'And whoever is forced by hunger' and that is like compulsion to eat carrion or drink wine. He said: 'It is hunger, and its ruling is that if he refrains from consuming it until he is killed, he will be sinful by refraining from what is permissible, and this type of rukhsa becomes like one who kills himself.'
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definition and categorization of the Islamic legal concepts of azima (original determination) and rukhsa (concession). The text first summarizes that azima includes fard (obligatory) and wajib (necessary) — noting that these categories have already been discussed. Then it defines rukhsa linguistically as ease and facility, and technically as the turning of a ruling from difficulty to ease due to a valid excuse in the person. The core of the passage is the division of rukhsa into two types. The first type is a concession to perform an act while the original prohibition remains intact in itself. The text gives examples: uttering words of disbelief under coercion while the heart is firm in faith, or insulting the Prophet, destroying property, or killing a person unjustly (when forced). In these cases, the act is still prohibited in essence, but the person is excused due to the extreme duress, similar to how a pardon in criminal law does not make the crime lawful. The second type is where the act becomes actually permissible (mubah) in the person's regard due to necessity, such as eating carrion when starving. Here the prohibition is lifted. The text emphasizes that if a person refrains from such permissible necessity and dies, he is sinful like a suicide. The reasoning is that the Sharia intends preservation of life, so refusing the concession becomes a violation.

Key Terms
عزيمة (azima): The original ruling that is not made easier due to an excuse; includes fard and wajib.
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession that turns a ruling from difficulty to ease due to a valid excuse.
إكراه (ikrah): Compulsion that removes moral responsibility for a prohibited act under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the first type of rukhsa from the second type according to this passage?
A) In the first type, the act becomes permissible; in the second, it remains prohibited.
B) In the first type, the act remains prohibited but is excused; in the second, the act becomes permissible.
C) In both types, the act becomes permissible, but the excuse differs.
D) In the first type, the act becomes wajib; in the second, it becomes haram.

Key Takeaway
Azima is the original ruling; rukhsa is a concession with two types: one retains prohibition (excused), the other makes the act permissible.
